Brown</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>Real-estate mogul Nick Candy's superyacht, 11.11, has hit the market for $71 million.&#160;</li>\n<li>The 207-foot yacht sports room It's because the ship is simply too small.&#160;</p><p>Candy, who's selling his yacht <a href=\"https://y.co/yacht/11-11\">11.11</a> for $71 million through yacht company Y.CO, is looking to upgrade to something a bit more spacious, he told <a href=\"https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2020-08-19/nick-candy-is-selling-11-11-yacht-for-71-million\">Bloomberg</a> recently.</p><p>\"I want to build a bigger yacht,\" Candy said. \"Later in life, people contract their lives; at this age, I'm still expanding.\"</p><p>Apparently, six bedrooms, multiple decks, pool areas, a nursery, a full-blown spa, and several bars isn't quite enough for the businessman. But Candy's extremely high standards aren't terribly surprising, given that he made his fortune selling some of the priciest and most extravagant real estate imaginable.&#160;</p><p>Take a closer look at 11.11 and all of its amenities below.</p>\n<div id=\"slideshow\">\n\tNick Candy — a British real-estate mogul who made a fortune on ultra-luxury homes — is selling his gargantuan superyacht for $71 million.\n\t<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f528df3e6ff30001d4e737a?format=jpeg\" alt=\"8. 11.11 Landscape View_JB\" height=\"1650\" width=\"2500\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>11.11.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Jeff Brown</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure>\n\t\n\tAlthough the 207-foot yacht is extravagant by most people's standards, Candy is looking to upgrade to something a bit larger, he told Bloomberg.\n\t<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f528e0ce6ff30001d4e737f?format=jpeg\" alt=\"11.11 Landscape   Jeff Brown\" height=\"1667\" width=\"2500\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>11.11.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Jeff Brown</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure>\n\t\n\t\"I want to build a bigger yacht,\" Candy told the outlet. \"It's like anything in life. usinessinsider?i=t80qNKoh3CA:y6L7vzP1q2I:gIN9vFwOqvQ":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx12/cdn96@2020_1/2020/09/06/14-05-25-767_e46564edbd28c813.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/businessinsider/~4/t80qNKoh3CA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx8/cdn83@2020_5/2020/09/06/14-05-49-640_1aea07f8053f0f19.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1599401096956},{"createdTime":"2020-09-06 22:04:57","updatedTime":"2020-09-06 22:04:57","title":"30 college towns that could face economic ruin if schools don't reopen or have to close again this fall","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/college-towns-economy-schools-reopen-fall-coronavirus-2020-8","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f4ff1a4e6ff30001d4e6b5c?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Montana State University\" height=\"1335\" width=\"1780\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>Classes begin for fall semester at Montana State University on August 17, 2020 in Bozeman, Montana.\n<p class=\"copyright\">William Campbell/Getty Images</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>Some college students are returning to campus for their <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/fewer-us-colleges-committing-to-in-person-fall-reopening-classes-2020-7\">fall semester</a>.</li>\n<li>Whether universities decide to have in-person classes or a hybrid model, college towns where students usually make up a large share of the town's population may be greatly affected.</li>\n<li>Business Insider decided to look at colleges that have a large number of undergraduates to determine which towns may be most economically vulnerable during the upcoming school year.&#160;&#160;</li>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">Visit Business Insider's homepage for more stories</a>.</li>\n</ul><p>Some college students across the country have already started their fall semesters, whether it be <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/fewer-us-colleges-committing-to-in-person-fall-reopening-classes-2020-7\">in-person or online</a>. As some students choose to take <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/us-colleges-higher-education-financial-struggles-pandemic-online-classes-2020-5\">online courses or are not interested in returning to college</a>, this can affect the economy of towns dependent on college students.&#160;&#160;</p><p>Many colleges closed and transitioned to remote learning in March, causing many to report <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/us-colleges-higher-education-financial-struggles-pandemic-online-classes-2020-5\">budget shortfalls</a>. Colleges are also estimating more financial losses for the current fiscal year. For instance, <a href=\"https://today.oregonstate.edu/news/oregon-state-board-trustees-approve-covid-19-resumption-plan-discuss-growing-budget-gap\">Oregon State University</a> projects a budget shortfall of $217 million, and undergraduates make up about 33.7% of the population in the university's home town of Corvallis.</p><p><a href=\"https://statements.cornell.edu/2020/20200702-additional-measures.cfm\">Cornell University</a>&#160;also estimates an overall budget shortfall of $210 million, even with a hybrid fall semester. The <a href=\"https://news.cornell.edu/stories/2020/04/leaders-discuss-budget-measures-forum\">Ithaca campus</a> of Cornell University already reported a loss of $45 million for the previous fiscal year.&#160;</p><p>There are many factors that contribute to the economies of college towns, including students spending money in the local economy and supporting businesses that employ workers. The <a href=\"https://www.bostonfed.org/publications/new-england-public-policy-center-regional-briefs/2020/college-towns-and-covid-19-the-impact-on-new-england.aspx\">Federal Reserve Bank of Boston</a> analyzed college towns in New England and found 45% of wages and 38% of jobs in these towns and cities come from colleges and universities.</p><p>Additionally, <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/small-businesses-in-college-towns-suffer-close-down-without-students-2020-6\">businesses</a> that usually rely on students bringing in revenue have also been negatively affected by campus closures these past few months. <a href=\"https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2020-08-21/what-the-pandemic-is-doing-to-college-towns\">Bloomberg CityLab</a> interviewed a hospitality company in State College, Pennsylvania, where the main campus of Penn State University is located. Curtis Shulman, the director of operations for the company, said 60% of their revenue occurs during the academic year and 20% alone comes from football weekends.&#160;&#160;</p><p>Business Insider decided to look at the college towns that would be most affected if students don't return for in-person classes or if the university with the largest number of undergrads in the town have to move to a fully remote semester.</p><p>To figure out which college towns are the most economically vulnerable, we based our ranking on undergraduate enrollment as a share of the town or city's population. To do this, we looked at the 200 colleges and universities with the largest number of degree- or certificate-seeking undergraduates based on fall 2018 college data from the <a href=\"https://nces.ed.gov/ipeds/use-the-data\">National Center for Education Statistics' Integrated Postsecondary Education Data System (IPEDS.)</a>&#160;We focused on schools that are mainly four-year, bachelor-earning schools.</p><p>We then calculated the share of these undergraduates in the population of the towns and places where these large campuses are located. We used 2018 population estimates of incorporated places from the <a href=\"https://www.census.gov/data/tables/time-series/demo/popest/2010s-total-cities-and-towns.html\">US Census Bureau</a> and focused on those with at least 30,000 residents.</p><p>IPEDS has specific enrollment data based on whether a student is taking distance classes or not. Since we want to estimate the share of students living in the town or city, we decided to look at the total number of undergraduates who are taking either some or no distance classes, rather than all-online courses, as a share of the population.</p><p>Although many of the colleges that made the top of our list are reopening for the fall semester, a few are choosing hybrid models or going fully remote. This includes <a href=\"https://newbrunswick.rutgers.edu/2020status\">Rutgers University-New Brunswick</a> where many of the classes will be held online and <a href=\"https://president.msu.edu/communications/messages-statements/2020_community_letters/2020-08-18-plans-change.html\">Michigan State University</a>, which only recently decided students should learn online.</p><p>The following are 30 college towns that may be the most negatively impacted if students of the largest college campus in the town don't return or have to leave campus again, based on the share of residents of the towns that are undergrads. We included both the share of students who are normally either fully on-campus or only taking some distance-based courses and the share who are taking no distance courses.</p>\n<div id=\"slideshow\">\n\t30. rx6/cdn31@2020_1/2020/09/06/14-05-47-368_6cb45d28ab3c445b.webp","https://i.insider.com/5f4fe8887ed0ee001e25d131?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx32/cdn53@2020_6/2020/09/06/14-05-48-760_1af1bff513e17b8b.webp","https://i.insider.com/5f4028c442f43f001ddfe70d?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx24/cdn99@2020_4/2020/09/06/14-05-49-973_5994b2a0c8236d9a.webp","https://i.insider.com/5f4fe7f6e6ff30001d4e6b19?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx29/cdn60@2020_1/2020/09/06/14-05-45-655_4aedb9ed77f653e1.webp","https://i.insider.com/5f4fe7d2e6ff30001d4e6b16?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx13/cdn51@2020_4/2020/09/06/14-05-42-642_bb8a0dbb246d2b1e.webp","https://i.insider.com/5f4fe7aae6ff30001d4e6b15?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx18/cdn7@2020_5/2020/09/06/14-05-44-234_2141f2424e6aee26.webp","https://i.insider.com/5ebec063aee6a877d22ff0f2?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx33/cdn16@2020_5/2020/09/06/14-05-44-608_3cf9870cc434d97a.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?d=yIl2AUoC8zA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx19/cdn76@2020_4/2020/09/06/14-05-49-381_cc3ef43fc8ff5801.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=hd6bTaBhanA:p7JoDrvJbo4:F7zBnMyn0Lo":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn40@2020_3/2020/09/06/14-05-51-868_7b7e37e38684e875.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=hd6bTaBhanA:p7JoDrvJbo4:V_sGLiPBpWU":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx6/cdn27@2020_1/2020/09/06/14-05-26-035_73c86d6b425f2149.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?d=qj6IDK7rITs":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn71@2020_5/2020/09/06/14-05-44-327_740defb435ea442b.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=hd6bTaBhanA:p7JoDrvJbo4:gIN9vFwOqvQ":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx31/cdn100@2020_4/2020/09/06/14-05-29-986_f60b65abfbc168c6.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/businessinsider/~4/hd6bTaBhanA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn99@2020_2/2020/09/06/14-05-52-534_f323f4dbbcf2a52f.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1599401097048},{"createdTime":"2020-09-06 22:04:56","updatedTime":"2020-09-06 22:04:56","title":"Governments should take some of their education budget and send money directly to parents to help with the switch to online learning","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/parents-education-funding-school-kids-education-online-classroom-2020-9","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f514761e6ff30001d4e6f58?format=jpeg\" alt=\"homeschooling\" height=\"2214\" width=\"4429\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>\n<p class=\"copyright\">ASSOCIATED PRESS</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>During the pandemic, parents have been finding themselves working less and teaching more, as school closures have kept their children home.</li>\n<li>These parents deserve to get some of the governments education funding to help ease the transtition.</li>\n<li>We need to put our kids in the forefront of the conversation, and let parents decide their educational paths.</li>\n<li>Karol Markowicz is a writer living in Brooklyn. .</li>\n<li>This is an opinion column. The thoughts expressed are those of the author.</li>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">Visit Business Insider's homepage for more stories</a>.</li>\n</ul><p>\"What are you doing about school?\" It's a question every parent in America has heard or answered this question in recent months: \"What are you doing about school?\"</p><p>We all pretend there's a good choice or a real answer. But as schools across the country plan an all-remote opening or a hybrid model with some in-person learning coupled with online learning, most parents know there is not.</p><p>What kids are doing about school depends very much on the wealth of their parents, now more than ever. But should it?</p><h2>An educational class divide</h2><p>With the upcoming school year still very much in flux, parents across the country will have to step in to pick up the educational slack left by school systems in disarray.&#160;</p><p>Parents will be forced to make an impossible choice: pause their careers and play administrative assistant to their kids while they learn via computer. Or don't and leave their kids on their own with potentially harmful consequences.&#160;</p><p>Some parents are setting up so-called \"pods\" to have their children either do <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/schools-choose-between-bad-online-learning-and-unsafe-classrooms-2020-7\">distance learning</a>, or actual homeschool, alongside a small group of other children. The idea is to give their kids some social interaction while mimicking at least some aspects of school.</p><p>The rich, of course, have many options. Their pods will likely consist of an in-person tutor who will work with the group to complete their schoolwork as they watch live instruction or videos from their actual school. Some will have the tutor design the curriculum themselves and form their own homeschool.</p><p>Other wealthy families will <a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2020/08/01/style/wealthy-rich-parents-coronavirus-schools-hamptons.html\">stay at their summer homes</a> if schools are opening there. Or they'll have the choice of private schools which have a <a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2020/07/16/upshot/coronavirus-school-reopening-private-public-gap.html\">far better chance</a> than public schools of opening in the fall.</p><p>But for the vast majority of families, this school year will be an educational disaster. The achievement gap between rich and poor kids <a href=\"https://www.economist.com/graphic-detail/2020/07/27/the-pandemic-is-widening-educational-inequality\">is expected to widen significantly</a>.&#160;</p><p>To fight this educational inequality, a growing movement is demanding that if schools don't open that the money spent per student be returned to the parents to use as they see fit. If parents don't like the plan proposed by their public school, why should they not be able to take their child's funds and spend that money on the education of their choice?</p><h2>Giving parents flexibility</h2><p>This month, Senator Rand Paul <a href=\"https://www.paul.senate.gov/sites/default/files/page-attachments/SCHOOLAct.pdf\">introduced</a> the Support Children Having Open Opportunities for Learning (SCHOOL) Act which would send the federal funds spent on schools directly to parents to spend as they wish. It's a good idea but it's likely not enough.&#160;</p><p>Corey A. DeAngelis, Director of School Choice at the Reason Foundation, told me these federal funds make up about 9% of total education spending. If all federal funds were diverted directly to parents, families in the northeast of the country — which has the highest per student spending in the country with an average of <a href=\"https://www.census.gov/newsroom/press-releases/2020/school-system-finances.html\">nearly $20,000</a> — would see around $1,800. That's certainly a start but nowhere near enough&#160; for parents to effectively supplement their child's lacking education.&#160;</p><p>And parents in other regions would see even less. Parents in the South — where funding per child averages around $10,000 — would only receive $900.</p><p>Given the limited amount of federal funding, much of the interesting work on directly funding \"students, not systems,\" as DeAngelis says, is happening on the state level.&#160;</p><p>In Colorado, their legislature asked Gov. Jared Polis to convene a special session specifically to discuss educational concerns. The&#160; policy proposal by Colorado Republicans called \"Safe Learning Choices\"<a href=\"https://co.chalkbeat.org/2020/7/28/21345397/colorado-republicans-special-session-homeschooling-pods\"> argues</a> families are \"entitled to all or a portion of their child's per-pupil revenue to access the educational resources they need to thrive during the pandemic.\"</p><p>DeAngelis told me \"State constitutions require governments to provide 'free' public education. It's hard for states to say they're doing that if they aren't even reopening schools.\"</p><p>With many teachers asking for medical accommodations, which will mean only getting a portion of their pay under the Family Medical Leave Act (FMLA) or the Americans with Disabilities Act, schools will see some savings. Instead of diverting any of those funds to other programs, they must be passed on to parents to give them educational options. Local and state governments have to find the money as well. Education is mandatory and if it's not being provided by the public schools parents should be able to get it elsewhere.</p><h2>We already know we need more options</h2><p>No one wants to see kids left behind because their parents couldn't afford to skip work to help them with distance learning. We've <a href=\"https://www.washingtonpost.com/outlook/coronavirus-homeschooling-will-hurt-students-badly/2020/03/27/f639882a-6f62-11ea-b148-e4ce3fbd85b5_story.html\">already seen the harm</a> caused by just a few months of this kind of schooling and studies show this set up can <a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2018/01/19/business/online-courses-are-harming-the-students-who-need-the-most-help.html\">leave many students behind</a> .&#160;</p><p>During the Spring, reports from parents about how distance learning was failing children were largely dismissed. After all, we were in a time where our tagline was \"two weeks to flatten the curve.\" Everyone thought it was temporary.&#160;</p><p>&#160;But as those two weeks kept getting extended provided, it was clear that many school systems were unprepared for the massive online shift.. I spoke to parents, at some of the best public schools in New York City, who did not have one instance of live online instruction during the spring semester.</p><p>Parents shouldn't be forced into another year of potentially lacking schooling just because they don't have the money for a private tutor or tuition for a private school. Schools which are not providing the service for which they are being paid should not continue to receive full funding. It shouldn't only be rich kids who have options.&#160;</p><h2>Taking politics out of the decision</h2><p>Some politicians may balk because the idea of school choice is seen as a conservative position, but the challenge right now is to depoliticize the issue of school funding for the good of the children. In normal times that would be an impossible task, but the unprecedented circumstances mean there's a chance.&#160;</p><p>Democrats can help shape the debate instead of shutting it down. They can help answer questions on how these funds can be used. What accreditation should be required for a teacher hired using this money? How can homeschooling parents prove how this money gets spent?&#160;</p><p>They can also force a time limit on these funds. Give parents access to these funds in the short term and see how it goes. If children thrive, why would we go back to the old system? If they don't, we can have an end date to the plan and return money to the schools.&#160;</p><p>Parents won't wait for their government to do the right thing for them. School districts around the country are already in the situation of asking parents to not un-enroll their children. Falls Church City Public Schools in Virginia <a href=\"https://www.fccps.org/article/287320?org=fccps\">sent a letter</a> to parents <a href=\"https://www.fccps.org/article/287320?org=fccps\">saying</a> they are experiencing \"a rise in families deciding to leave the system until we are either back in person or there is a vaccine to fight the virus\" and that \"If there is an exodus of students from FCCPS, the funding for our schools will decrease.\"&#160;</p><p>Similarly, The Denver Public Schools Board of Education <a href=\"https://www.dpsk12.org/board-of-education-statement-on-learning-pods-and-their-impact-on-the-community\">discouraged</a> parents from unenrolling their children and forming pods. \"It's also important to recognize the impact of unenrolling children from their school and/or hiring private teachers. We are deeply concerned about the pods' long-term negative implications for public education and social justice.\"</p><p>They're not wrong about these negative implications but their solution — to keep all children in non-optimal learning conditions — is untenable. Parents who can afford private tutors will still opt out or use their money to gain access to the tools to make online learning work for them. We need to afford less privileged children that same opportunity.&#160;</p><p>COVID-19 has forced us to make difficult choices and so many parents have lopsidedly bore the brunt of that. We need to put children at the forefront of the school conversation and let their parents decide how best to educate them. Fund students not pre-COVID ideas on what schools should be.</p><div class=\"read-original\">Read the original article on <a He hasn't been seen for more than 5 years.","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/journalist-sentenced-death-isis-not-seen-over-five-years-2020-9","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f524382e6ff30001d4e7115?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Farhad Hamo\" height=\"1000\" width=\"2000\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>Farhad Hamo.\n<p class=\"copyright\">One Free Press Coalition</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>A freelance journalist captured by ISIS and sentenced to death hasn't been seen since his colleague saw him taken away from a prison more than five years ago.</li>\n<li>Farhad Hamo and Massoud Aqeel were abducted and imprisoned by ISIS in 2014 while on their way to interview a local politician.</li>\n<li>Aqeel said he was tortured and was constantly threatened with being killed, before he was released after nine months and made it to Germany as a refugee.</li>\n<li>But Hamo has not been seen since Aqeel saw him removed from the prison in March 2015.</li>\n<li>His brother continued to look for him among ISIS hostages but he has not been found.</li>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">Visit Business Insider's homepage for more stories</a>.</li>\n</ul><p>A freelance journalist in Syria who was captured by ISIS and sentenced to a beheading hasn't been seen since he was taken away from a prison five and a half years ago.</p><p>Even though ISIS no longer holds physical territory, and has greatly diminished in prominence, many of its victims are still unaccounted for.</p><p>Farhad Hamo was abducted by ISIS members in December 2014 while on his way to interview a local politician for the Kurdish broadcaster Rudaw TV.</p><p>He was abducted while with Massoud Aqeel, a 23-year-old English literature student that was working as a cameraman on the same job.</p><p><a href=\"https://www.independent.co.uk/news/world/europe/syrian-refugee-scouring-germany-jihadists-isis-masoud-aqil-prisoner-tortured-rudaw-a7351086.html\">Aqeel told The Independent</a> in a 2016 interview that they were driving down a highway when they saw it was blocked.</p><p>He said six militants armed with M16 rifles, grenades, and suicide vests forced the vehicle to stop.</p><p>\"ISIS were waiting on the highway,\" he said. \"I don't know if they were waiting for us or if they were there to catch anyone.\"</p><p>He said they lied and said they were oil workers, but the militants saw media equipment in the car. He said that one man sat in the car and told them to drive into ISIS territory, or else he would blow up himself and the vehicle.</p><p>Aqeel was imprisoned for nine months, and he said he was tortured and threatened with execution for being a Kurdish journalist.</p><p>\"They tortured us and interrogated us,\" he said. \"They beat us with iron bars, cables or wood, tying us to the ceiling by our hands.</p><p>\"Every two or three hours one of the guards would come in and tell us 'we will cut off your heads, we will bury you alive.'\"</p><p>Aqeel was released in September 2015, returning to a war zone and later coming to Germany as a refugee.</p><p>But Hamo has not been seen Aqeel saw him taken from prison in Raqqa, Syria, in March 2015, he told the <a href=\"https://www.onefreepresscoalition.com/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">One Free Press Coalition</a>, a collection of news organizations that covers media freedom.</p><p>What happened to Hamo next is not known.</p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f5242c6e6ff30001d4e710b?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Raqqa ISIS Syria\" height=\"1333\" width=\"2000\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>A man whose home was destroyed rests on a trip to get water for his family in Raqqa, Syria, in October 2018.\n<p class=\"copyright\">DELIL SOULEIMAN/AFP via Getty Images</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>An ISIS court sentenced both Hamo and Aqil to death by beheading in December 2014, <a href=\"https://cpj.org/data/people/farhad-hamo/\">according to the Committee to Protect Journalists</a>.</p><p>They were put in a jail for 40 days before Abu Ayoub, a senior ISIS figure, requested that they be put in solitary confinement in Raqqa's jail, Aqeel told Rudaw in July 2016, according to the committee.</p><p>The ISIS prison in Raqqa was able to hold thousands of people, <a href=\"https://news.sky.com/story/inside-islamic-states-prison-of-death-in-fallen-raqqa-11090263\">Sky News reported in 2017</a>, after the group's hold on the region diminished.</p><p>It reported that the prison had \"a tiny box for extreme punishment,\" metal beds where people were strapped down and executed, a room where people were hung on hooks and flogged, and weight machines that bent people's bodies.</p><p>But in 2019, Aqeel's brother said he believed Hamo may still have been be alive.</p><p>Rudaw had<a href=\"http://www.rudaw.net/english/kurdistan/100720162\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\" aria-label=\"External link: originally reported\">&#160;reported</a> in 2016 that Hamo was released in October 2015, but <a href=\"https://www.rudaw.net/english/middleeast/syria/15122018\">in an update in 2018 said</a> that he was still missing.</p><p>\"Despite multiple calls for his release by Rudaw Media Network and other outlets, his fate remains unknown. Neither his family nor colleagues have new information on his whereabouts,\" it reported.</p><p>Aras Hamo told the Committee to Protect Journalists in February 2019 that he was searching for Farhad near Baghouz, a village in eastern Syria that was the last ISIS enclave in the country.</p><p>\"Everything seems to suggest that Farhad is alive, but we don't have evidence or real confirmation,\" he said.&#160;</p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5cee382911e205141b75f8a2?format=jpeg\" alt=\"FILE PHOTO: Islamic state fighters and their families walk as they surrender in the village of Baghouz, Deir Al Zor province, Syria March 12, 2019. REUTERS/Rodi Said/File Photo\" height=\"1795\" width=\"2546\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>ISIS fighters and their families surrender in the village of Baghouz, Syria, in March 2019.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Reuters</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>He said that Syrian Democratic Forces soldiers, who were backed by the US, said that there were 400 hostages, including journalists, being held there.</p><p>He said in April that authorities were trying to find Hamo and other hostages in Baghouz.&#160;</p><p>Hamo is still missing now.</p><p><em>Insider is covering Hamo's case as part of <a href=\"https://www.onefreepresscoalition.com/\" data-analytics-module=\"body_link\" data-analytics-post-depth=\"100\">The One Free Press Coalition</a>, which raises awareness of the world's persecuted journalists.</em></p><div class=\"read-original\">Read the original article on <a border=\"0\"></img></a>\n</div><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/businessinsider/~4/4QH4vvAAkaM\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Sun, 06 Sep 2020 09:00:00 +0000","feedId":22,"bgimg":"https://i.insider.com/5f52437d7ed0ee001e25d7a8?format=jpeg","linkMd5":"72026fb7a431b009fe1a5a72166f5cf6","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx/cdn87@2020_3/2020/09/06/14-05-25-834_1268ac47f355d7d6.webp","destWidth":2000,"destHeight":1500,"sourceBytes":83916,"destBytes":83916,"author":"sbaker@businessinsider.com (Sinéad Baker)","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://i.insider.com/5f524382e6ff30001d4e7115?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx6/cdn67@2020_4/2020/09/06/14-05-50-361_a21616d2cd78a465.webp","https://i.insider.com/5f5242c6e6ff30001d4e710b?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx16/cdn68@2020_4/2020/09/06/14-05-29-409_b89321eba4c655ad.webp","https://i.insider.com/5cee382911e205141b75f8a2?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn88@2020_3/2020/09/06/14-05-46-907_36c31db1db96d5c5.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?d=yIl2AUoC8zA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx19/cdn76@2020_4/2020/09/06/14-05-49-381_cc3ef43fc8ff5801.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=4QH4vvAAkaM:8QlslN5koag:F7zBnMyn0Lo":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx27/cdn47@2020_6/2020/09/06/14-05-47-663_ab0b740f1b799de7.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=4QH4vvAAkaM:8QlslN5koag:V_sGLiPBpWU":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx21/cdn32@2020_3/2020/09/06/14-05-28-602_b3c58187427a5e14.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?d=qj6IDK7rITs":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn71@2020_5/2020/09/06/14-05-44-327_740defb435ea442b.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=4QH4vvAAkaM:8QlslN5koag:gIN9vFwOqvQ":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx32/cdn71@2020_3/2020/09/06/14-05-47-688_781db1a527bee7ed.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/businessinsider/~4/4QH4vvAAkaM":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx10/cdn67@2020_5/2020/09/06/14-05-44-599_def9288b07b3411b.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1599401096948},{"createdTime":"2020-09-06 22:04:57","updatedTime":"2020-09-06 22:04:57","title":"We lose about 30 minutes of sleep each work night, and sleeping in on the weekends doesn't always help","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/lose-30-minutes-sleep-work-nights-weekend-sleeping-in-bad-2020-9","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5e6a89f0235c18278923fc06?format=jpeg\" alt=\"dog face sleeping\" height=\"3264\" width=\"4352\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>Sleeping extra on the weekends doesn&#39;t make up for a bad week of rest.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Shutterstock</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>A recent study from Sweden followed the sleep cycles of 100 people, aged 60 to 71, over two years as they transitioned into retirement.</li>\n<li>They found that participants had chronic partial sleep deprivation during the work week, clocking about 2.5 hours per week less than the ideal amount of sleep.</li>\n<li>If you're trying to develop healthier sleeping habits, try to stick to a regular sleep schedule, even on weekends, to keep your biological clock running smoothly.</li>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">Visit Business Insider's homepage for more stories</a>.</li>\n</ul><p>For many of us, work often competes for time with sleep — which is why many of us look forward to the weekend for a chance to \"catch up\" on sleep. But how much sleep is lost on days when we work? Our <a href=\"http://dx.doi.org/10.1111/jsr.13157\">latest research shows</a> that we get about 30 minutes less sleep than we would ideally need on each night of the working week.</p><p>We followed 100 people aged from 60 to 71 over two years, covering their transition into retirement. We measured their sleep on three separate occasions, with one year in between, and compared the sleep habits while they were working against when — and for how long — they slept after retirement.</p><p>After retirement, we found that every day was like a weekend — at least when it came to how long people slept for. Sleep duration increased, but only on weekdays, from 6.5 to seven hours a night on average. This meant retired people got about an equal amount of sleep every night of the week.</p><p>The amount of sleep people tended to get on their weekends while still in work seemed to be their preferred sleep duration, rather than \"catch-up\" sleep. If weekend sleep was prolonged to compensate for the working week's sleep loss, we would have expected a drop after retirement (when there's no sleep loss to compensate for) — but this wasn't the case.</p><p>Given that participants' weekend sleep was their preferred sleep duration, weekend lie-ins will not compensate for sleep lost on weekdays while working. This means that our study participants had chronic partial sleep deprivation when they were working, of about 2.5 hours each week.</p><p>While adults are recommended to get <a href=\"https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC4434546/\">at least seven hours</a> per night for optimal health, sleep needs vary both between people and as we age. We need less sleep when we are older than when we are younger.</p><p>Different people need different amounts of sleep, which makes it hard to estimate what constitutes \"too little\" sleep for any given individual, but <a href=\"https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1046/j.1365-2869.2003.00337.x\">other</a> <a href=\"https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/12683469/\">studies</a> have in experiments found that getting only six to seven hours of sleep affects attention and reaction time negatively compared to getting eight to nine hours of shuteye. This performance drop remained, even after getting a full night's sleep three days in a row.</p><p>Partial sleep deprivation as a result of work can continue for years, which is why the accumulated effects needs to be considered. Sleeping less than seven hours on a regular basis is related to increased risk for <a href=\"https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC4434546/\">various health conditions</a>, including diabetes, stroke, and depression. It's also <a href=\"https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC4434546/\">associated with</a> impaired immune system function, as well as increased risk of accidents.</p><h2>Social jetlag</h2><p>Not only did sleep duration change with retirement, but people also went to bed later and woke later. Getting rid of the alarm clock seemed to be what drove the increase, as retired people went to bed about half an hour later and woke up an hour later on average during weekdays compared to when they were working.</p><p>Going to bed in time to get plenty of sleep before getting up for work is not always easy — especially for the majority of the population who have a late \"<a href=\"https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S1087079207000895\">biological clock</a>\". This means they naturally prefer to go to sleep later and wake up later than people with an early biological clock.</p><p>Those with a late biological clock also have a tendency to postpone their bed and wake times on weekends more than others, which unfortunately sets their biological clock even later — making it hard to go to bed early on Sunday and even harder wake up early on Monday morning.</p><p>When our biological clock is out of sync with the social clock (which is the timetable imposed on us by society) it can result in \"<a href=\"https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC6784249/\">social jetlag</a>.\" Social jetlag acts a bit like regular jetlag, and can make us feel down and tired. It's also associated with higher risk for <a href=\"https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC6784249/\">metabolic disorders and depressive symptoms</a>.</p><p>Longer and more stable sleep across the week could, at least partly, explain why so many people experience <a href=\"https://journals.lww.com/epidem/Fulltext/2011/07000/Is_Retirement_Beneficial_for_Mental_Health__.20.aspx\">improved mental health</a> and drastically <a href=\"https://www.bmj.com/content/341/bmj.c6149\">lower levels of fatigue</a> after retirement.</p><p>But even though sleep patterns became more stable after retirement, people still went to bed and woke up around half an hour later on weekends compared to weekdays. This hints that other social factors — such as visiting with friends — also affect when and how much we sleep.</p><p>We also found that retired participants with a full-time working partner changed their sleep timing to a smaller extent than the rest, highlighting that sleep is social, as opposed to a purely individual phenomenon.</p><p>But there are some things you can do yourself to adjust your sleep patterns more to work and avoid \"social jetlag\" on Monday morning, including making sure you get plenty of daylight in the mornings. Morning light pushes our biological clock backwards, making it easier to fall asleep at night. However, the opposite is also true, so bright light should be avoided in the evenings and bedrooms should be dark.</p><p>It also helps to prioritize your sleep and keep a more regular sleep schedule, even on weekends. Allow yourself some extra time in bed on weekend mornings if you need it, but try to avoid throwing your weekend sleep schedule off too much in order to stay away from the vicious cycle of sleep loss and social jetlag.</p><p>That being said, our study suggests that work generates sleep loss and hinders people from sleeping in line with their natural rhythm. But just as <a href=\"https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S1087079215000891?via%3Dihub\">later school start times</a> are an effective way to improve sleep in adolescents, later (or flexible) start times at work could potentially have the same effect for working people — and may mean people won't have to wait until retirement to get enough sleep.<!-- End of code. If you don't see any code above, please get new code from the Advanced tab after you click the republish button. The page counter does not collect any personal data. More info: https://theconversation.com/republishing-guidelines --></p><p><em><a href=\"https://theconversation.com/profiles/johanna-garefelt-1147910\">Johanna Garefelt</a>, PhD candidate in public health at the Stress Research Institute, <a href=\"https://theconversation.com/institutions/stockholm-university-1019\">Stockholm University</a></em></p><div class=\"insider-raw-embed\"><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://counter.theconversation.com/content/144890/count.gif?distributor=republish-lightbox-advanced?format=jpeg\" alt=\"The Conversation\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>\n<p class=\"copyright\"></p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure></div><p>This article is republished from <a href=\"https://theconversation.com\">The Conversation</a> under a Creative Commons license. 22:04:56","updatedTime":"2020-09-06 22:04:56","title":"3 recent discoveries have upended scientists' understanding of how dogs age, navigate, and perceive human speech","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/dogs-navigate-magnetic-field-understand-speech-like-humans-2020-8","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f448ce442f43f001ddfec9a?format=jpeg\" alt=\"husky dog doing trick giving paw shaking hand\" height=\"3644\" width=\"4859\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>A husky puppy shakes hands with its owner.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Shutterstock</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\"><li>Three recent discoveries have changed scientists' understanding of the nature of dogs.</li><li>One study found that <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/calculating-dog-age-human-years-2020-7\">young puppies age much faster than young humans do</a>, so the common rule of thumb that one \"dog year\" is equivalent to seven \"human years\" is wrong.</li><li>Other recent research reveals that dogs understand human speech in the same way we do.</li><li>Findings also suggest dogs may use Earth's magnetic field to navigate.</li><li><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">Visit Business Insider's homepage for more stories</a>.</li></ul><p>It often seems impossible to know what's going on in Fido's head as he lounges on his dog bed.</p><p>But a handful of new studies offer surprising new insights about how our canine companions age, perceive human speech, and find their way home.</p><p><a href=\"https://www.nature.com/articles/s41598-020-68821-6\">A study</a> published last month showed that dogs understand verbal communication just like we do: They parse out tone then meaning as separate aspects of human speech.&#160;</p><p>Dogs may also use Earth's magnetic field like a compass to find their way home, other recent <a href=\"https://elifesciences.org/articles/55080\">research revealed</a>.</p><p>And <a href=\"https://www.cell.com/cell-systems/fulltext/S2405-4712(20)30203-9\">another study</a> published this summer found that puppies age much faster than older dogs do — which means you're probably underestimating your dog's human-age equivalent.</p><p>Taken together, these recent discoveries may change how you understand your pet.</p><h2>We've been underestimating our dogs' ages</h2><p>Dogs live an average of 12 years. Human life expectancy, by contrast, is <a href=\"https://ourworldindata.org/life-expectancy\" data-analytics-module=\"body_link\" data-analytics-post-depth=\"20\">at least five times that</a>, which is why many people go by the&#160;common rule of thumb that&#160;<a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/is-a-dog-year-equal-to-7-human-years-2014-10#:~:text=The%20seven%2Dyear%20rule%20is,at%20least%20once%20a%20year.%22\" data-analytics-module=\"body_link\" data-analytics-post-depth=\"20\">one \"dog year\" is equivalent to seven \"human years</a>.\"</p><p>But that one-to-seven ratio is wrong, <a href=\"https://www.cell.com/cell-systems/fulltext/S2405-4712(20)30203-9\">researchers found</a> — it's a <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/calculating-dog-age-human-years-2020-7\">misunderstanding of how dogs' aging processes</a> compare to those of humans. Instead, according to a July study, genetic evidence suggests that Labrador puppies and other young dogs age faster than their older counterparts.</p><p>\"What's surprising is exactly how old a 1-year-old dog is — it's like a 30-year-old human,\" Trey Ideker, a co-author of that study, <a href=\"https://eurekalert.org/pub_releases/2020-07/cp-asm070220.php\" data-analytics-module=\"body_link\" data-analytics-post-depth=\"40\">said in a press release</a>.&#160;</p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f448c6b89aff80028ab7ac8?format=jpeg\" alt=\"lab puppy\" height=\"3497\" width=\"4662\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>An 8-week-old golden Labrador husky mix sleeps on a cushion.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Angela Auclair/Getty</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>But after that first year or two, dogs age more slowly as time passes — or rather, their cells do. So for each year older a dog gets, the corresponding increase in \"human years\" gets smaller. A 12-year-old Labrador is 70 human years old, the study found.</p><p>Ultimately, in order to calculate your dog's human-age equivalent, you'll need a calculator. The researchers' formula is: A dog's human age = 16 ln * your dog's age + 31. (The ln refers to the natural log of a number.)</p><h2>Dogs understand speech in the same way we do</h2><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5e4573ca96eee670365a4d82?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Westminster Dog Show\" height=\"1500\" width=\"2000\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>A dog gets groomed at the Westminster Dog Show.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Anneta Konstantinides/Insider</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>When humans hear someone speak, our brains divide the work if processing that verbal communication between the left and right hemispheres. First, the right hemisphere focuses on parsing out the speaker's underlying tone, then the left hemisphere processes the meaning of what we've heard.</p><p>Researchers discovered in 2014 that dogs' brains divvy up the task of <a href=\"https://theconversation.com/dog-brains-process-human-speech-in-the-same-way-we-do-35075\">speech processing</a> in the same way, between their left and right hemispheres, though the scientists weren't sure of the order in which that happened.</p><p>The <a href=\"https://www.nature.com/articles/s41598-020-68821-6\">recent study</a>, however, found that dogs understand tone first, then meaning, in the same order humans do. The authors examined the brain activity of 12 pet dogs — six border collies, five golden retrievers, and one German shepherd — using a functional magnetic resonance imaging (fMRI) machine.</p><p>They had the dogs listen to known praise words like \"clever,\" \"well done,\" and \"that's it,\" as well as unknown words like \"as if\" and \"yet\" in both praising and neutral tones. The data showed that the dogs processed \"simpler, emotionally loaded cues\" like tone first, then \"more complex, learnt cues,\" Attila Andics, one of the co-authors, <a href=\"https://www.eurekalert.org/pub_releases/2020-08/eelu-sph072720.php\">said in a press release</a>.</p><h2>Dogs use the magnetic field to navigate</h2><p>Dogs have an uncanny ability to find their way home using their sense of smell. But that's not the only navigational tool at their disposal: Pups can also orient themselves using Earth's magnetic field, according to a <a href=\"https://elifesciences.org/articles/55080\">June study</a>.</p><p>Birds, <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/gray-whales-stranded-during-solar-storms-cant-navigate-properly-2020-2\">whales</a>, and dolphins also use the same sixth sense, called magnetoreception, to plot their long migrations.</p><p>\"The magnetic field may provide dogs with a 'universal' reference frame, which is essential for long-distance navigation, and arguably, the most important component that is 'missing' from our current understanding of mammalian special behavior and cognition,\" the authors of the recent study wrote.</p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f453c4342f43f001ddfee01?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Two_Wire_Fox_Terriers\" height=\"2332\" width=\"3482\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>Two wire-haired Fox Terriers.\n<p class=\"copyright\"><a href=\"https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/File:Two_Wire_Fox_Terriers.jpg\" target=\"_blank\">Alicia Nijdam/Wikimedia Commons</a></p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>The researchers used GPS trackers to study how 27 dogs navigated over the course of 662 expeditions between 2014 and 2017 in the Czech Republic. They chose hunting breeds like terriers and dachshunds, which are trained to find prey like foxes in dense woodlands, then find their way back to their owners.</p><p>The researchers found that after the dogs were released into the woods, the animals typically retraced their steps back to their release point by following their own scent. But in one-third of the expeditions, the dogs took a different way home: On those trips, most of the dogs started off by running for 65 feet along Earth's north-south axis — even if the direction of their destination didn't align with that orientation.</p><p>This \"compass run\" helps the dogs figure out where magnetic north and south are, and where they are in relation to those points, according to the study authors.</p><p>\"This run is instrumental for bringing the mental map into register with the magnetic compass and to establish the heading of the animal,\" they wrote.</p><p>Kateřina Benediktová, one of the study authors,&#160;<a href=\"https://www.livescience.com/dogs-sense-earth-magnetic-field.html\">told Live Science</a> that unlike hunting dogs, a \"human would most probably get lost without a compass and a map if roaming over comparable distances in unfamiliar forested areas.\"</p><div class=\"read-original\">Read the original article on <a (Aylin Woodward)","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://i.insider.com/5f448ce442f43f001ddfec9a?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx15/cdn35@2020_6/2020/09/06/14-05-49-944_1c139d3d852530a6.webp","https://i.insider.com/5f448c6b89aff80028ab7ac8?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx33/cdn94@2020_5/2020/09/06/14-05-26-045_6792b5bd548a103d.webp","https://i.insider.com/5e4573ca96eee670365a4d82?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx18/cdn55@2020_3/2020/09/06/14-05-47-566_8ada89490c891d93.webp","https://i.insider.com/5f453c4342f43f001ddfee01?format=jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn59@2020_3/2020/09/06/14-05-37-748_ba538733850935d3.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?d=yIl2AUoC8zA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx19/cdn76@2020_4/2020/09/06/14-05-49-381_cc3ef43fc8ff5801.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=MvDhEud1g-Y:UN-iQE7QXbc:F7zBnMyn0Lo":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx15/cdn67@2020_1/2020/09/06/14-05-37-733_415df41598da60df.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=MvDhEud1g-Y:UN-iQE7QXbc:V_sGLiPBpWU":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn87@2020_6/2020/09/06/14-05-51-386_fa904b263f20c321.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?d=qj6IDK7rITs":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn71@2020_5/2020/09/06/14-05-44-327_740defb435ea442b.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/businessinsider?i=MvDhEud1g-Y:UN-iQE7QXbc:gIN9vFwOqvQ":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx/cdn71@2020_4/2020/09/06/14-05-42-984_0a89b7f710438d5f.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/businessinsider/~4/MvDhEud1g-Y":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx1/cdn56@2020_5/2020/09/06/14-05-50-461_78a78e7a63933df7.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1599401096948},{"createdTime":"2020-09-06 22:04:56","updatedTime":"2020-09-06 22:04:56","title":"Stop assigning journalists of color the 'racism beat'","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/journalists-of-color-assigned-stories-racism-beat-reckoning-black-2020-8","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f3d397bcdf25a001e84e225?format=jpeg\" alt=\"blm protest\" height=\"1131\" width=\"2263\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>July 26: A protester holds a sign that says, &#34;Black Lives Matter&#34; as the crowd of hundreds pass the Jumbotron of the American Flag in Times Square\n<p class=\"copyright\">Ira L. Black/Corbis via Getty Images</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>Journalists of color have a unique understanding of racism.</li>\n<li>However, they are often assigned the \"racism beat,\" which is inherently a taxing thing to cover.</li>\n<li>Journalists of color are more than just the color of their skin, and should be treated as such.</li>\n<li>Neha Maqsood is a Pakistani journalist.</li>\n<li>This is an opinion column. The thoughts expressed are those of the author.</li>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">Visit Business Insider's homepage for more stories</a>.</li>\n</ul><p>As a writer of color, I possess a distinct power. Through my identity as a Pakistani-Muslim, certain lived experiences have facilitated my writing on topics like racism, immigration, microaggressions, and Islamophobia. These subjects are not wholly accessible to a white writer who hasn't felt their effects firsthand.&#160;My authority on these topics has undoubtedly helped propel me at the start of my career, leading to multiple bylines with different publications.</p><p>However, in my early years as a journalist, I was also inadvertently pigeonholed onto the \"race beat\" – white editors and major publications would chiefly commission me to write on issues dealing with race that had already been covered extensively by writers of color before me.</p><p>Despite my frustration, the race beat also cemented the belief that as a brown woman, I was obligated to ensure that the unreported stories and events of communities of color were brought to light.</p><p>This was an opportunity to chronicle the systemic disparity and prejudice that was widespread across the globe. Through my writing, I attempted to appeal to the average white person to view things from the perspective of a person of color – to step into our shoes and see how different life was from this side. Ultimately, however, I realized that race was not&#160; just a 'beat' – it was a lived experience. And having to retell these traumatic stories would not only be taxing, but also an immense sacrifice to my mental health.</p><p>Following the latest uprisings connected to the <a href=\"https://blacklivesmatter.com/about/\">Black Lives Matter movement</a>, there has been an abundance of features, op-eds and think pieces examining the tragic stories about police brutality.&#160;However, once these pieces are published across a wide range of publications, writers of color, particularly black writers, are continually asked to relive traumatic experiences by writing about topics which seem pretty cut and dry — racism is bad, white supremacy still reigns supreme. The end.</p><p>The responsibility of racial coverage has consistently been put solely on writers of color, granting white writers the license to bypass the subject. However, there is an inherent need to instill racial consciousness across the spectrum of all writers. Race is an intersectional phenomenon, and even white writers must be educated on how to integrate it into the narrative of stories. Whilst it is true that the expertise of writers of color can permit them to craft unique connections, it does not justify the burden of putting race reporting purely on them.</p><p>Race is entangled with inequality in politics, healthcare, business, housing, environment and other avenues. A 2016 research article published by <a href=\"https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/full/10.1177/1464884916663599\">Sage Journal</a> found that white reporters' history of remaining 'objective' when reporting on communities of colour had not benefited said communities in the slightest.</p><p>Objectively laying out the facts, instead, allowed for 'a narrative of ignorance, stereotyping, and racist framing'; remaining passive to the perspectives of marginalized communities perpetuates a vicious cycle where people of colour are seen as simply belonging to the binaries of good and bad. White writers must examine the trends, analyse the data and immerse themselves in a community which isn't their own to comprehend the more intricate reasons behind systemic bias.</p><h2><strong>Exploitative personal essays</strong></h2><p>Beyond news reporting, editors often rely on personal essays from writers of color to highlight critical issues. but in many cases these essays are not only achingly uncomfortable to write but also often verge on being exploitative.</p><p>In 2019, I had penned a very <a href=\"https://metro.co.uk/2019/12/30/racism-forced-dad-uk-23-years-later-driven-11976714/\">personal essay</a> for a prominent publication in the UK detailing my parents' immigration from Pakistan to the UK in the 1980's. My parents had moved in pursuit of a better life for their future children to a place where they believed success was based on merit and came to anyone who worked hard enough for it. However, after a few years of being subjected to blatant racism (from violent threats to being called 'Paki') and the second class treatment, my parents ultimately returned to Pakistan.</p><p>Through this piece, I conveyed that Britain had unfortunately forgotten to acknowledge its own colonial past and its xenophobic present. But following the publication of the piece, there was extraordinary backlash from predominantly white people. There were the classic responses including, 'Why don't you just go back to your own country' or the subtle: 'Paki's will always be our slaves'.</p><p>As journalists, we generally stay detached from our stories, but this was a different ballgame. This wasn't really my story; it was the story of my parents who had graciously shared their experiences with me knowing full well it's significance in South Asian history. Having white people trivialize the experiences of writers of color and invalidate the prejudice they feel, can make the entire process of writing seem not only soul wrenching but worthless .</p><p>I still often get commissioned on topics related to the 'race beat', but I mostly decline them. Most recently, I got asked to write an analysis piece on the <a href=\"https://www.washingtonpost.com/outlook/the-kashmir-crisis-isnt-about-territory-its-about-a-hindu-victory-over-islam/2019/08/16/ab84ffe2-bf79-11e9-a5c6-1e74f7ec4a93_story.html\">Kashmir conflict</a> and the brutality invoked upon Kashmiri-Muslim's under the banner of Hindu nationalism, but I refused to do so. Thousands of writers before me have covered this story, and it is unlikely that my words will manifest actual change. At best, the piece may get a few retweets, a handful of angry comments and then be permanently archived in internet history. How many more angry think pieces are required to be written before sustainable and effective change is undertaken?</p><p>When journalists or literary writers write about such distressing issues, there is an unconscious hope that our work will at the least strike up a conversation and at the most, will induce a public response or lead to a policy change. But more often than not, our work seems to come to a standstill.</p><p>I will still continue to write about race and cover underrepresented communities and their stories, but I also want the media world to view me as more than just the color of my skin. Writers of color contain multitudes; stories about love, light, family, sacrifice, and compassion. It's high time that our stories be accepted in spite of the color of our skin and equally because of it.</p><div class=\"read-original\">Read the original article on <a 22:04:56","updatedTime":"2020-09-06 22:04:56","title":"A Wall Street chief strategist details 8 ways today's economy mirrors the 1980s recession recovery — and how it can lift stocks even higher","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/stock-market-outlook-inflation-scare-drive-recession-recovery-investment-strategist-2020-9","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f5131e3e6ff30001d4e6ea6?format=jpeg\" alt=\"NYSE stock market\" height=\"1000\" width=\"2000\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>\n<p class=\"copyright\">Wang Ying/Xinhua/Getty</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul><li><strong>Today's inflation concerns mirror those seen after the 1980 economic slump. And James Paulsen, chief strategist at The Leuthold Group, sees the trend as a boon for markets.</strong></li><li><strong>The inflation surge seen four decades ago kicked off \"a fabulous 'disinflationary,' two-decade performance in both the economy and the stock market,\" he wrote in a recent note.</strong></li><li><strong>With policymakers setting the stage for a rise in inflation, the US is on track to usher in a similarly fruitful expansion, the strategist added.</strong></li><li><strong>Detailed below are the eight reasons Paulsen sees the current inflation scare leading to a thriving economy and strengthened bull market.</strong></li><li><strong><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?utm_source=markets&utm_medium=ingest?utm_source=markets&utm_medium=ingestutm_source=msn.com&utm_medium=referral&utm_content=msn-slideshow&utm_campaign=bodyurl?utm_source=markets&utm_medium=ingestts&#38;utm_medium=ingest\">Visit the Business Insider homepage for more stories</a>.</strong></li></ul><hr><p>Inflation fears today mirror those seen during the 1980 recession. That similarity may be what saves markets from their own lofty valuations, according to James Paulsen, chief strategist at <a href=\"https://leutholdgroup.com/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">The Leuthold Group</a>.</p><p>Stagnant price growth has plagued the US economy for years, and the coronavirus pandemic only worsened the problem. The Federal Reserve cut rates to zero, kicked off an unprecedented asset purchase program, and boosted the nation's money supply in an effort to keep inflation stable through the downturn.</p><p>Investors now fear such conditions will stifle a recovery from the current recession, but history suggests the opposite, according to the strategist.</p><p>The 1980s inflation fear spike sparked \"the beginning of a fabulous 'disinflationary,' two-decade performance in both the economy and the stock market,\" Paulsen said. The initial scare motivated a coordinated effort to right the economy. Such a solution will likely emerge once the coronavirus is contained and policymakers focus on stabilizing inflation.</p><p>Detailed below are the eight reasons Paulsen sees today's inflation scare giving way to a thriving economy and surging stock market.</p><p><em><strong>Read more:</strong> <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/stock-market-crash-bofa-september-meltdown-risks-technical-indicators-2020-9\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Bank of America lays out the under-the-radar indicators showing that huge swaths of the stock market are 'running on fumes' — and warns a September meltdown may just be getting started</a></em></p><h2>Demographic shift</h2><p>The US working-age population's decade-long annualized growth reached a record-low 0.3% this year, slumping in a downtrend similar to that seen starting in 1980. The past drop eventually reversed and led to a growth in the working-age population, a shift likely to repeat itself in the coming years, Paulsen said.</p><p>\"The size of the Gen-X age group is set to exceed the Baby Boomers in a few years,\" he wrote in a note.</p><p>That shift is partially behind the housing market's strength through the pandemic, as Gen-X Americans reach \"prime household-formation years.\" As the group ages further, they'll reach \"peak spending years\" and accelerate the nation's economic bounce-back, Paulsen added.</p><h2>Immigration vs inflation</h2><p>The country is also likely to pad its demographic trends by allowing for greater immigration over the next few years, the strategist said. Doing so \"could make underlying economic-resource growth far stronger than currently anticipated.\"</p><p><em><strong>Read more:</strong> <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/us-investing-championship-strategy-matthew-caruso-contender-secret-to-return-2020-8\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">US Investing Championship hopeful Matthew Caruso landed a 382% return in the first half of 2020. He shares the unique twist he's putting on a classic trading strategy — and 3 stocks he's holding right now.</a></em></p><h2>Turning the trade tide</h2><p>The economic rebound could be accelerated further should the US aspire to finally reach a trade surplus, Paulsen said. Trade balance data released Thursday showed the country <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/us-trade-deficit-july-widest-point-since-2008-coronavirus-economy-2020-9\">running a deficit of $63.6 billion</a>, its widest since 2008. A policy of enforcing fair-play trade among economic superpowers could improve the US's trade competitiveness and lift the economy, according to the strategist.</p><p>Boosting export activity would have a direct and powerful effect on national gross domestic product. Paulsen noted that, in a hypothetical scenario where the balance shifts from a 1% deficit to a 1% surplus, US GDP would improve an entire 2%.</p><h2>Closing the output gap</h2><p>The nation's output gap — a measure of the under-utilization of economic resources — sits at a post-war low. Such a disparity leaves \"considerable room for improvement\" when the pandemic ends and the US puts its resources back to work, Paulsen said.&#160;</p><p><em><strong>Read more:</strong> <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/stocks-to-buy-25-owned-outperforming-women-fund-managers-gs-2020-9\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">GOLDMAN SACHS: Female portfolio managers are outperforming their male counterparts so far in 2020. These are the 25 stocks they own the most compared to men.</a><br></em></p><h2>Permanently low-cost</h2><p>Companies battened down the hatches to ride out the pandemic, and that cost-cutting could end up speeding up the nation's rebound. Lowered breakeven points will yield greater profits when activity picks up, Paulsen said. The trend will also justify stocks' historically high prices as investors pay up for \"uncommon earnings leverage,\" he added.</p><h2>Recipe for productivity</h2><p>The latter two predictions come together in Paulsen's forecast for a surge in productivity. \"Hyper-efficient companies\" and the strong potential output-gap improvement suggests economic productivity \"could surprise to the upside\" throughout the recovery, he wrote.</p><h2>Poised to spend</h2><p>Households are unusually prepared to drive the economy out of its slump compared to past downturns, according to Paulsen. Americans spent more than a decade paying off debts, refinancing, raising their net worths, and growing more cost-efficient. Homes now tout a savings rate close to 20%, \"an uncommonly liquid status quo\" that stands to ignite a strong new expansion, the strategist wrote.</p><p>\"How much could economic growth be bolstered should household-sector attitudes turn optimistic about the future?\" he added.</p><p><em><strong>Read more:</strong> <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/stock-market-crash-expert-says-hypervalued-market-set-to-plunge-2020-9\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">'Never been so extreme': A renowned stock bear says today's 'hypervalued' market implies the worst market returns in history — and expects a 66% crash from today's levels</a></em></p><h2>Government backing</h2><p>Lastly, Paulsen sees the government's \"amazingly aggressive\" monetary and fiscal policies buttressing the nation's economic rebound. The Federal Reserve recently overhauled its policy framework to better control inflation as it expects to hold interest rates close to zero through 2022. Congress remains in a deadlock over new stimulus, but economists still expect a package to arrive before the November election.</p><p>So long as the government and the Fed keep their relief efforts intact, Paulsen expects the backdrop to usher in an expansion to rival even the most bullish outlooks.</p><p>\"Just as the restrictive policies employed in 1980 helped to break the inflation surge, it seems probable today's unique and massively accommodative economic policies will quicken future economic growth more than thought,\" the strategist wrote.</p><p><em><strong>Now read more markets coverage from Markets Insider and Business Insider:</strong></em></p><p><em><strong><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/tesla-stock-sale-why-to-buy-retail-investors-tasha-keeney-2020-9\" data-analytics-position=\"7\" data-analytics-module=\"\">One of Tesla's biggest bulls on Wall Street breaks down how the company's $5 billion stock sale could benefit retail investors — and explains why the electric-car giant would still be cheap at $1,400 per share in 2024</a></strong></em></p><p><em><strong><a href=\"https://markets.businessinsider.com/news/stocks/quantumscape-spac-ipo-market-ev-bill-gates-backed-battery-company-2020-9-1029560551\" data-analytics-position=\"2\" 22:04:56","updatedTime":"2020-09-06 22:04:56","title":"The hour-by-hour pandemic routine that's calmed my anxiety and kept me focused","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/hour-by-hour-pandemic-routine-calms-anxiety-kept-me-focused-2020-9","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f5250457ed0ee001e25d820?format=jpeg\" alt=\"Jersey Griggs lead\" height=\"1739\" width=\"2319\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>From picking flowers to journaling, writer Jersey Griggs has been nailing down a daily self-care routine.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Jersey Griggs</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li><a href=\"https://jersey-griggs.github.io/\" data-analytics-module=\"summary_bullets\" data-analytics-post-depth=\"0\" data-uri=\"538e73c7e5867cb97d1b8827c62df5cd\">Jersey Griggs</a>&#160;is a writer specializing in lifestyle topics who lives on the coast of Maine with her husband and their rescue dog.</li>\n<li>Pre-pandemic, she had an inconsistent self-care routine &#8212; it was anything from watching a Bravo show with a glass of wine to meditating in the morning.</li>\n<li>But the insularity of the pandemic has given her the opportunity to nail down an actual daily routine.</li>\n<li>From journaling to yogic sleep, here's the routine she's been using to take care of herself.</li>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">Visit Business Insider's homepage for more stories</a>.</li>\n</ul><p>Over the course of the last decade, my self-care routine has been inconsistent. Some days, self-care meant rising before the sun to meditate for 30 minutes. Other days, it meant eating a bowl of pasta on the couch, with a glass of wine and a Bravo show.</p><p>But with the arrival of COVID-19, everything changed, and I was forced to live a more insular life. Although being stuck in my house had many downsides, there was one positive development: without the distraction of the outside world, it was easier to implement a daily self-care routine.&#160;</p><p>It didn't take long to realize that taking time for myself, even during a workday, was imperative for my well-being during this stressful time. Here's the routine that works for me:&#160;</p><h2><strong>7:00 am</strong>: I get out of bed, gratefully accept a cup of tea from my husband, and begin to write in my journal.&#160;</h2><p>Today I have a lot going on in my head, and I pour my thoughts, feelings, worries, and ideas into three pages in my notebook. By the time I am finished, I feel lighter, unbound by my perceived limitations, and I have a better sense of direction for the day.</p><p>Next, I set a daily intention using oracle cards. During the pandemic, I started working with <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Wild-Offering-Oracle-52-Card-Giving/dp/1401954766\">The Wild Offering Oracle</a>; the deck is imbued with hope and usually offers a positive perspective, even when the world feels gloomy. I shuffle the deck, fan the cards, and choose one at random. I record the intention in my journal and keep the card face up throughout the day to serve as a reminder.</p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f524b3f7ed0ee001e25d7e6?format=jpeg\" alt=\"IMG 1146\" height=\"1739\" width=\"2319\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>The author choosing a card.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Jersey Griggs</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>As a rule, I'm not allowed to check my phone until I've completed this routine. Starting my day with thoughtful introspection — instead of going straight to the news or social media — allows me to check in with myself without bias. When I begin my day clear-headed and centered (and without any negative distractions from outside forces) my mental health is rewarded.</p><h2><strong>10:30 am</strong>: After working for an hour and a half, I take a mid-morning break to meditate.</h2><p>This habit began at the onset of the pandemic, when I was invited by a friend to meditate with the <a href=\"https://sfdharmacollective.org/\">San Francisco Dharma Collective</a>. The community-led sangha offers a virtual <a href=\"https://sfdharmacollective.org/upcoming-events/\">morning sit</a> every day at 7:30 PST/10:30 EST, and the unguided group meditation is open to all.&#160;</p><p>Meditating with a group at a designated time has allowed me to cultivate a more consistent practice; eventually, I became accustomed to meditating on my own as a way to break up the morning.</p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f524b80e6ff30001d4e7156?format=jpeg\" alt=\"IMG 1169\" height=\"2274\" width=\"3024\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>The author meditating.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Jersey Griggs</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>This morning, I am feeling creatively blocked. Instead of berating myself for being stuck, I give myself a literal break. As I settle onto my meditation pillow — which is just around the corner from my desk — I choose to put my frustration aside for 15 minutes.&#160;</p><p>I set a timer on my phone, close my eyes, and drop into the quiet. As I focus on my breath, I'm able to clear the clutter in my brain; it feels like a return to myself.&#160;</p><p>Shortly into the meditation, an idea pops into my head. For a moment, I recognize the solution to my problem, but I ignore it by focusing on my breath. When the timer goes off, I open my eyes and return to my desk with a fresh idea, eager to work.&#160;</p><h2><strong>12:30 pm</strong>: After eating lunch, I allow myself 30 minutes of free time to spend outside, sort of like recess during school.</h2><p>Before the pandemic, I used to rush back to work, worried about getting everything done. But during quarantine, I discovered that I benefited greatly from any time spent outside, even during the workday.</p><p>What I do during this time varies from walking the dog to weeding the vegetable garden, but one thing remains the same — it is time I carve out to enjoy life away from my desk.</p><p>Today, I pick a bouquet of flowers from my garden to drop off at my neighbor's house, who recently lost her dog to cancer. Picking and arranging flowers is simultaneously soothing and joyful, and it's a new favorite pastime.</p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f524c7ee6ff30001d4e7166?format=jpeg\" alt=\"IMG 0200\" height=\"2268\" width=\"3023\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>The bouquet of flowers.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Jersey Griggs</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure>&#160;I admire a bee drinking a sunflower's nectar and stop to watch a butterfly float past, finding myself grateful the pandemic gave my husband the time to dig these new flower beds.<p>When I return to my computer, I feel rested and rejuvenated. Instead of detracting from my profession, I've found that this scheduled free time translates into more focus and greater clarity during my workday.&#160;</p><h2><strong>8:00 pm</strong>: Today is Wednesday, which means it's time for <a href=\"https://www.mindfulbreathworkproject.com/bookings-checkout/yoga-nidra\">Yoga Nidra</a> with Becca Stoltz, creator of the <a href=\"https://www.mindfulbreathworkproject.com/\">Mindful Breathwork Project</a>.</h2><p>Also known as \"yogic sleep,\" Yoga Nidra is a guided practice that takes you to a place of deep consciousness, a state somewhere between wake and sleep.&#160;</p><p>In recent months, it has become an invaluable part of my weekly self-care routine. It's been helpful in releasing the physical discomforts and mental anxieties that often accumulate over the course of a week.&#160;</p><p>Before signing onto Zoom, I brush my teeth and put on my pajamas, as if I'm getting ready for bed. Then I climb under the covers and arrange myself into a comfortable position. Lying on my back, I have an extra pillow under my knees and an eye-pillow over my face. My dog climbs into bed to snuggle at my feet, providing an added dose of comfort.</p><figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5f524ccd7ed0ee001e25d801?format=jpeg\" alt=\"IMG 1114\" height=\"3024\" width=\"4032\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>The author practicing Yoga Nidra.\n<p class=\"copyright\">Jersey Griggs</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><p>As Stoltz begins to speak in a soothing, rhythmic tone, she asks everyone to remain curious about any sensations, emotions, and thoughts that may arise throughout the course of the class.&#160;</p><p>With this gentle reminder, I register that the current state of the world has me on constant high-alert and I mentally switch off the part of my brain that tells me to be fearful. As we go through a systematic relaxation of the body, I realize how much I've been gripping throughout the day and I allow myself to let go completely.</p><p>Over the course of the next hour, Stoltz continues to use different breathing exercises, meditations, and visualization techniques to help me go deeper and deeper into a state of relaxed consciousness.&#160;</p><p>By the end of the class, I feel as if I'm floating on a cloud, without a care in the world. I stay in bed and drift off into a deep and restful sleep — which is perhaps my favorite self-care practice of them all.</p><div class=\"read-original\">Read the original article on <a
